<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Yttrium</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>

				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with element <a href="/wiki/Ytterbium" title="Ytterbium">ytterbium</a> <i>(Yb)</i>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 39</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Yttrium,&#160;&#160;<sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">39</span></sub>Y</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a href="/wiki/File:Yttrium_sublimed_dendritic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Yttrium sublimed dendritic and 1cm3 cube.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Yttrium_sublimed_dendritic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/220px-Yttrium_sublimed_dendritic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" width="220" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Yttrium_sublimed_dendritic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/330px-Yttrium_sublimed_dendritic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Yttrium_sublimed_dendritic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/440px-Yttrium_sublimed_dendritic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4952" data-file-height="3368" /></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ffc0c0">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span></span>/</a></span></span>&#x20;<wbr />&#8203;<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">IT</span>-ree-əm</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_yttrium" title="Template:Infobox yttrium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_yttrium" title="Template talk:Infobox yttrium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_yttrium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Yttrium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Y</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 39. It is a silvery-metallic <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a> chemically similar to the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a> and has often been classified as a "<a href="/wiki/Rare-earth_element" title="Rare-earth element">rare-earth element</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-IUPAC1_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUPAC1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Yttrium is almost always found in combination with lanthanide elements in <a href="/wiki/Rare-earth_mineral" title="Rare-earth mineral">rare-earth minerals</a>, and is never found in nature as a free element. <sup>89</sup>Y is the only stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a>, and the only isotope found in the <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">Earth's crust</a>.
</p><p>In 1787, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Axel_Arrhenius" title="Carl Axel Arrhenius">Carl Axel Arrhenius</a> found a new mineral near <a href="/wiki/Ytterby" title="Ytterby">Ytterby</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and named it <i><a href="/wiki/Gadolinite" title="Gadolinite">ytterbite</a></i>, after the village. <a href="/wiki/Johan_Gadolin" title="Johan Gadolin">Johan Gadolin</a> discovered yttrium's oxide in Arrhenius' sample in 1789,<sup id="cite_ref-Krogt_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krogt-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Anders_Gustaf_Ekeberg" title="Anders Gustaf Ekeberg">Anders Gustaf Ekeberg</a> named the new <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Yttria" class="mw-redirect" title="Yttria">yttria</a></i>. Elemental yttrium was first isolated in 1828 by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most important uses of yttrium are <a href="/wiki/LED" class="mw-redirect" title="LED">LEDs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphor" title="Phosphor">phosphors</a>, particularly the red phosphors in television set <a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tube" title="Cathode ray tube">cathode ray tube</a> (CRT) displays.<sup id="cite_ref-Cotton_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Yttrium is also used in the production of <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">electrodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolytes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electronic_filters" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic filters">electronic filters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Superconductor" class="mw-redirect" title="Superconductor">superconductors</a>, various medical applications, and <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">tracing</a> various materials to enhance their properties.
</p><p>Yttrium has no known <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological</a> role. Exposure to yttrium compounds can cause <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_disease" title="Respiratory disease">lung disease</a> in humans.<sup id="cite_ref-osha_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osha-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
